Intention for Travel vs Residence; Ship Dispute
Clarifying that intention suffices for residence but requires action for travel, with a ship example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that intention alone suffices for residence but requires action for travel, and apply it to determine the ruling when a resident's prayer is interrupted by travel.

Core Idea
For travel, intention must be accompanied by action; for residence, mere intention suffices.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: the role of intention versus physical action in establishing travel or residence status. It then applies this distinction to a practical scenario involving a ship, where a resident's prayer is disrupted by the ship's movement and a change of intention. The intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani provides a window into how scholars weigh conflicting evidences and adopt precautionary rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule about intention
Read the first part of the passage: intention alone suffices for residence, but for travel it must be accompanied by action (like walking or riding). Note the analogy with fasting: intending to break the fast does not break it until one actually eats or drinks.
This principle is foundational for determining when travel licenses apply.
2
Understand the ship scenario
The scenario: a person starts prayer as a resident (obligated to pray four rakats) in a ship at the edge of the sea. During the prayer, the wind moves the ship, and he intends to travel. The question: should he complete four rakats or shorten to two?
The prayer began under residence, but travel occurred during the prayer.
3
Analyze the conflicting factors
Two factors compete: the original obligation of four (because the prayer started as a resident) and the prevention by travel (which normally allows shortening). Note how each imam weighs these factors: Abu Yusuf prioritizes the obligation of four; Muhammad prioritizes the actualization of travel.
This is the core of the ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the tarjih method: ihtiyat
The author states that they preferred the ruling that necessitates four out of precaution (ihtiyat). Understand that ihtiyat is a tarjih principle that selects the more cautious ruling to ensure the act is valid and free from doubt.
In Hanafi methodology, ihtiyat is often used when proofs are balanced.
5
State the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's: the person completes the prayer as four rakats. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
If one follows Muhammad's view, the prayer would need to be repeated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما اكتفى بالنية في الإقامة واشترط العمل معها في السفر لما أن في السفر الحاجة إلى الفعل وهو لا يكفيه مجرد النية ما لم يقارنها عمل من ركوب أو مشي كالصائم إذا نوى الإفطار لا يكون مفطراً مالم يفطر، وفي الإقامة الحاجة إلى ترك الفعل وفي الترك يكفي مجرد النية كعبد التجارة إذا نواه للخدمة. وأشار المصنف إلى أن النية لا بد أن تكون قبل الصلاة ولذا قال في التجنيس : إذا افتتح الصلاة في السفينة حال إقامته في طرف البحر فنقلها الريح وهو في السفينة ونوى السفر يتم صلاة المقيم عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد لأنه اجتمع في هذه الصلاة مايوجب الأربع وما يمنع فرجحنا ما يوجب الأربع احتياطاً .اهـ.
And the author relied on intention alone in residence and required action with it in travel because in travel there is need for action, and mere intention is not sufficient unless action accompanies it (riding or walking), like a fasting person who intends to break his fast does not become a breaker of fast unless he actually breaks. In residence, the need is to leave action, and in leaving, mere intention suffices, like a trade slave if his owner intends him for service. And the author indicated that intention must be before the prayer, thus he said in al-Tajnis: If one opens the prayer in a ship while in the residence state at the edge of the sea, then the wind moves the ship while he is in it and he intends travel – he completes the prayer of a resident according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, because in this prayer there is gathered that which necessitates four and that which prevents, and we preferred that which necessitates four out of precaution.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute presented as expansion of a principle about intention. It first differentiates between residence and travel: for residence, mere intention (e.g., to reside 15 days) suffices to establish residence; for travel, intention alone is not enough – one must actually begin moving (walking, riding). The analogy with fasting illustrates that mental intent to break fast does not break it until actual consumption. Then it introduces a specific scenario from al-Tajnis: a person in a ship at the edge of the sea starts prayer as a resident (i.e., four rakat). During prayer, the wind moves the ship and he intends to travel. Does he complete four or shorten to two? Abu Yusuf says he completes four, Muhammad says he shortens. The author states that they (the Hanafi school) prefer Abu Yusuf's view as a precaution (ihtiyat) because two factors are present: one necessitates four (the intention of residence at the start) and one prevents (the travel during prayer). They choose the more cautious option requiring full prayer. The ruling is that the prayer is completed as four. The passage demonstrates that the Hanafi school uses istihsan and precaution to resolve conflicting evidences.

Key Terms
إقامة (iqaamah): The status of being a resident; praying complete four-rakat prayers and not availing travel licenses.
نية (niyyah): Intention; in this context, intention to travel or to reside. For residence, niyyah alone suffices; for travel, it must accompany action.
احتياط (ihtiyat): Precautionary preference; the author chooses the ruling that is more cautious to avoid potential invalidity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the ship scenario, which Hanafi imam's view is relied upon as mufta bihi?
A) Imam Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Imam Zufar

Key Takeaway
When conflicting evidences arise, the Hanafi school may adopt the more cautious (ihtiyat) ruling to ensure validity.

The Hanafi Criterion for Travel Distance: Three Days, Not Farsakhs
Debates the criteria for travel distance, including rest periods and farsakhs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for travel distance and explain why farsakhs are disregarded in favor of three days of moderate travel including rest.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school defines travel as three days of moderate travel; rest periods are included, and farsakhs are irrelevant.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the definition of travel distance (masafat al-safar) needed to qualify for concessions like qasr and iftar. The author clarifies that the sole criterion is three days of customary travel, inclusive of necessary rest stops, and that attempts to fix a farsakh measurement contradict the explicit hadith. We will trace the proof, the inclusion of rest, and the rejection of alternative views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its source
What is the Hanafi position on the minimal travel distance for concessions? Which hadith does the author rely on as proof?
The hadith prohibits women from traveling three days without a mahram, taken as the definition of travel.
2
Understand the inclusion of rest periods
The author explains that three days include time for rest (both for the traveler and animal). The correct view does not require traveling from dawn to dawn each day; even partial days count.
This refutes Fath al-Qadir's objection and is justified by necessity (darurah).
3
Analyze the rejection of farsakhs
The author argues that farsakhs are not considered because a rugged road may take three days but be less than 15 farsakhs; using farsakhs would deny the concession, contradicting the text.
Later scholars from Khwarazm issued fatwas on 15 or 18 farsakhs, but the author expresses amazement at this deviation.
4
Apply to different travel scenarios
The passage covers various cases: choosing longer roads, faster travel (courier/horse), sea travel with moderate winds, and very slow travel (oxcarts). In each, the three-day criterion is adapted to the mode of transport.
Customary travel (al-syr al-mu'tad) is the standard; people are referred to when in doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للاستراحة فلا يعتبر والمراد ثلاثة أيام من أقصر أيام السنة. وهل يشترط سفر كل يوم إلى الليل؟ اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه لا يشترط حتى لو بكر في اليوم الأول ومشى إلى الزوال ثم في اليوم الثاني كذلك، ثم في اليوم الثالث كذلك فإنه يصير مسافراً لأن المسافر لا بد له من النزول لاستراحة نفسه ودابته فلا يشترط أن يسافر من الفجر إلى الفجر لأن الآدمي لا يطيق ذلك، وكذلك الدواب فألحقت مدة الاستراحة بمدة السفر لأجل الضرورة. كذا في السراج الوهاج. وبه اندفع ما في فتح القدير لأن أقل اليوم إذا كان ملحقاً بأكثره للضرورة لم يكن فيه مخالفة للحديث المفيد للثلاثة كما أن الليل للاستراحة وهو مذكور في الحديث. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لا اعتبار بالفراسخ وهو الصحيح لأن الطريق لو كان وعراً بحيث يقطع في ثلاثة أيام أقل من خمسة عشر فرسخاً قصر بالنص، وعلى التقدير بها لا يقصر فيعارض النص فلا يعتبر سوى الثلاثة. وفي النهاية الفتوى على اعتبار ثمانية عشر فرسخاً. وفي المجتبى: فتوى أكثر خوارزم على خمسة عشر فرسخاً. اهـ. وأنا أتعجب من فتواهم في هذا وأمثاله بما يخالف مذهب الإمام خصوصاً المخالف للنص الصريح. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان: الرجل إذا قصد بلدة وإلى مقصده طريقان أحدهما مسيرة ثلاثة أيام ولياليها والآخر دونها فسلك الطريق الأبعد كان مسافراً عندنا. اهـ. وإن سلك الأقصر يتم وهذا جواب واقعة الملاحين بخوارزم فإن من الجرجانية إلى مدائق اثني عشر فرسخاً في البر، وفي جيحون أكثر من عشرين فرسخاً، فجاز لركاب السفينة والملاحين القصر والإفطار فيه صاعداً ومنحدراً. كذا في المجتبى. وذكر الأسبيجابي المقيم إذا قصد مصراً من الأمصار وهو ما دون مسيرة ثلاثة أيام لا يكون مسافراً، ولو أنه خرج من ذلك المصر الذي قصد إلى مصر آخر وهو أيضاً أقل من ثلاثة أيام فإنه لا يكون مسافراً، وإن طاف آفاق الدنيا على هذا السبيل لا يكون مسافراً. اهـ. وفي السراج الوهاج: إذا كانت المسافة ثلاثة أيام بالسير المعتاد فسار إليها على البريد سيراً مسرعاً أو على الفرس جرياً حثيثاً فوصل في يومين قصر. اهـ. والمراد بسير البر والجبل أن يكون بالإبل ومشي الأقدام، والمراد بالإبل إبل القافلة دون البريد. وأما السير في البحر فيعتبر ما يليق بحاله وهو أن يكون مسافة ثلاثة فيه إذا كانت تلك الرياح معتدلة وإن كانت تلك المسافة بحيث تقطع في البر في يوم في الجبل يعتبر كونها من طريق الجبل بالسير الوسط ثلاثة أيام، وإن كانت تقطع من طريق السهل فالحاصل أن المدة من أي طريق أخذ فيه، ولهذا عمم المصنف رحمه الله. وخرج سير البقر بجر العجلة ونحوه لأنه أبطأ السير كما أن أسرعه سير الفرس والبريد والوسط ما ذكرنا وفي البدائع: ثم يعتبر في كل ذلك السير المعتاد فيه وذلك معلوم عند الناس فيرجع إليهم عند الاشتباه.
For rest, it is not considered; the intended meaning is three days from the shortest days of the year. And is it required to travel each day until night? They differed, and the correct view is that it is not required, such that if he set out early on the first day and walked until noon, then on the second day likewise, then on the third day likewise, he becomes a traveler because the traveler must dismount to rest himself and his mount, so it is not required that he travel from dawn to dawn because a human cannot bear that, likewise animals. Thus the period of rest is annexed to the period of travel out of necessity. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. By this is refuted what is in Fath al-Qadir, because if the lesser part of the day is annexed to its greater part out of necessity, there is no violation of the hadith indicating three days, just as the night is for rest, which is mentioned in the hadith. And the author indicated that there is no consideration of farsakhs, and that is correct because if the road is rugged such that it is traversed in three days less than fifteen farsakhs, one shortens by textual evidence; and if based on farsakhs he would not shorten, which contradicts the text, so only the three days are considered. In al-Nihaya, the fatwa is on the consideration of eighteen farsakhs. In al-Mujtaba: the fatwa of most of Khwarazm is on fifteen farsakhs. End. And I am amazed at their fatwa in this and similar matters that contradict the madhhab of the Imam, especially contradicting the explicit text. In Fatawa Qadi Khan: If a man intends a town and to his destination there are two roads, one being three days and nights journey and the other less, and he takes the longer road, he is a traveler according to us. End. And if he takes the shorter, he completes. This is the answer to the incident of the sailors in Khwarazm: from al-Jurjaniyya to Mada'iq is twelve farsakhs by land, and in the Jayhun more than twenty farsakhs, so the ship passengers and sailors may shorten and break fast there, going upstream and downstream. Thus in al-Mujtaba. And al-Asbijaabi mentioned: A resident who intends a city among cities that is less than three days journey is not a traveler; and if he goes out from that city which he intended to another city that is also less than three days, he is not a traveler; and if he wanders the horizons of the world in this manner, he is not a traveler. End. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If the distance is three days by customary travel, and he travels to it via courier quickly or on a horse at a fast gallop and arrives in two days, he shortens. End. The meaning of land and mountain travel is by camel and walking on foot, and the meaning of camel is the caravan camel, not the courier. As for sea travel, what is appropriate to its condition is considered: that it be a distance of three days there when the winds are moderate; and if that distance is such that it is traversed by land in one day in the mountain, it is considered by the mountain route with moderate travel three days; and if it is traversed by the plain route, the conclusion is that the period is from whichever route he takes; therefore the author (may Allah have mercy on him) generalized. And he excluded travel by oxen pulling a wagon and the like because it is the slowest travel, just as the fastest is horse and courier travel, and the moderate is what we mentioned. In al-Bada'i': Then in all that, customary travel is considered, and that is known among people, so they are referred to when there is doubt.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition of travel distance (masafat al-safar) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author clarifies that the criterion is three days of travel, not farsakhs, and rest periods are included. The text begins by stating that rest stops are not counted separately; rather, the whole journey including rest is meant by 'three days.' The correct view, per al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, is that one does not need to travel from dawn to dawn each day; traveling from morning to noon on three consecutive days suffices, because humans and animals need rest. This is supported by necessity (darurah). The author refutes Fath al-Qadir's objection by noting that annexing the lesser part of the day to the greater part does not contradict the hadith. The author then affirms that the madhhab's position is to ignore farsakhs, because if a rugged road is covered in three days but is less than 15 farsakhs, the traveler shortens by text; if we used farsakhs, he would not shorten, contradicting the text. Later scholars in al-Nihaya and al-Mujtaba issued fatwas based on 18 or 15 farsakhs, but the author expresses amazement at this departure from the explicit text. He then cites Fatawa Qadi Khan on choosing a longer route, and al-Siraj al-Wahhaj on speeding making a journey shorter in time but still considered travel if the distance is three days by normal pace. Finally, he discusses sea travel and the criterion being three days with moderate winds, and excludes very slow travel like oxcarts. The passage concludes with al-Bada'i' noting that customary travel is known to people; thus they are referred to in case of doubt.

Key Terms
فرسخ (farsakh): A unit of distance, approximately 3 miles or 5.76 km; the text uses it as an alternative measurement for travel distance.
سير معتاد (syr mu'tad): Customary travel pace, typically by camel caravan, considered moderate.
مسافة السفر (masafat al-safar): The distance that makes one a traveler, defined as three days of moderate travel including rest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct criterion for travel distance in the Hanafi madhhab?
A) 15 farsakhs
B) 18 farsakhs
C) Three days of moderate travel including rest periods
D) Any distance covered in two days by fast travel

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines travel for concessions exclusively by a three-day journey of moderate pace including rest, not by any fixed mileage.

Qasr for Travelers: Obligation or Concession?
Explains that qasr is the original obligation for travelers, not a concession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qasr for travelers and explain why it is considered an azimah rather than a rukhsa, citing the supporting dalil.

Core Idea
Qasr is the original fard for the traveler—not a concession—so completing four rak'ahs is a sin.

In the Hanafi madhhab, the shortening of prayer (qasr) during travel is often misunderstood as a concession that the traveler may choose. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that qasr is actually the original obligation (azimah) for the traveler. Based on the hadith that prayer was originally prescribed as two rak'ahs and only increased for the resident, the traveler's duty remains two rak'ahs. Thus, praying four is a violation of the Sunnah. Let's unpack the reasoning and the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its label
Read the passage to find the ruling on qasr: what must the traveler pray? How is it labeled (azimah vs rukhsa)?
The passage states qasr is wajib; completing four is sinful.
2
Understand the refutation of the 'rukhsa' label
Why does al-Bada'i' say calling qasr a concession is wrong? Trace the argument: there is no change from a heavier ruling to a lighter one for the traveler.
Originally prayer was two for both; the increase was for the resident only.
3
Examine the dalil
The passage cites the hadith of Aisha. Identify the proof and how it establishes that the traveler's obligation is two rak'ahs.
The hadith shows the increase applied only to the resident.
4
Note the scope and the sunnah issue
What prayers does qasr apply to? What is the difference of opinion on sunnah prayers during travel?
Qasr applies only to fard; sunnahs are optional.
5
Contrast with other schools
How does the Shafi'i view differ? What is the practical consequence (thamarah)?
Shafi'i considers qasr a rukhsa; completing four is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث أعني حكم السفر فهو تغيير بعض الأحكام فذكر المصنف منها قصر الصلاة والمراد وجوب قصرها حتى لو أتم فإنه آثم عاص لأن الفرض عندنا من ذوات الأربع ركعتان في حقه لا غير، ومن مشايخنا من لقب المسألة بأن القصر عندنا عزيمة والإكمال رخصة. قال في البدائع: وهذا التلقيب على أصلنا خطأ لأن الركعتين في حقه ليستا قصراً حقيقة عندنا بل هما تمام فرض المسافر والإكمال ليس رخصة في حقه بل إساءة ومخالفة للسنة، ولأن الرخصة اسم لما تغير عن الحكم الأصلي بعارض إلى تخفيف ويسر ولم يوجد معنى التغيير في حق المسافر رأساً إذ الصلاة في الأصل فرضت ركعتين في حق المقيم والمسافر ثم زيدت ركعتين في حق المقيم كما روته عائشة رضي الله عنها فانعدم معنى التغيير في حقه أصلاً، وفي حق المقيم وجد التغيير لكن إلى الغلظ والشدة لا إلى السهولة واليسر والرخصة تنبيء عن ذلك فلم يكن رخصة حقيقة في حق المقيم أيضاً، ولو سمى فإنما هو مجاز لوجود بعض معاني الحقيقة وهو التغيير. اهـ. فعلى هذا لو قال في جواب الشرط صلى الفرض الرباعي ركعتين لكان أولى. وقيد بالفرض لأنه لا قصر في الوتر والسنن. واختلفوا في ترك السنن في السفر؛ فقيل الأفضل هو الترك ترخيصاً، وقيل الفعل تقرباً. وقال الهندواني: الفعل حال النزول والترك حال السير.
As for the third, meaning the ruling of travel, it is the change of some rulings. Among them the author mentioned the shortening of prayer, and the intended meaning is the obligation of shortening it, such that if he completes it, he is sinful and disobedient because the obligatory in the four-rak'ah prayers is two rak'ahs in his case, nothing else. And some of our scholars have labeled the issue as: shortening with us is an original obligation (azimah), and completing is a concession. In al-Bada'i', it is said: This labeling according to our school is wrong because the two rak'ahs in his case are not shortening in reality according to us; rather they are the complete obligatory of the traveler, and completing is not a concession in his case but rather a bad act and a violation of the Sunnah. And because concession is a name for what changes from the original ruling due to a contingent circumstance towards ease and leniency, but the meaning of change is not found in the case of the traveler at all, since prayer originally was obligated as two rak'ahs for both the resident and the traveler, then two rak'ahs were added for the resident as narrated by Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her); thus the meaning of change is entirely absent in his case. And in the case of the resident, the change is found but towards severity and hardship, not towards ease and leniency, and concession indicates that, so it was not a real concession in the case of the resident either. And if it is so called, it is only metaphorical because of the presence of some aspects of the real meaning, which is change. End. Therefore, if he were to say in answer to the condition: 'He prays the four-rak'ah obligatory as two rak'ahs,' that would be more appropriate. And he restricted it to the obligatory because there is no shortening in the witr and the sunnahs. And they differed regarding leaving the sunnahs in travel: some said the best is to leave them as a concession, others said to perform them as a means of drawing near. And al-Hinduwani said: perform them when stopping, and leave them when traveling.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage covers the third category of travel rulings: the change of certain ahkam, specifically the shortening of prayer (qasr al-salah). The author clarifies that in the Hanafi madhhab, qasr is an obligation (wajib) for the traveler, not a concession. If a traveler prays four rak'ahs, he commits a sin. Some scholars termed qasr an 'azimah (original obligation) and completing a rukhsa (concession), but al-Bada'i' refutes this: originally, prayer was prescribed as two rak'ahs for both resident and traveler; later, two were added for the resident. So the traveler's obligation remains two; there is no 'change' from a heavier to a lighter ruling. Therefore, qasr is not a concession; it is the original rule. For the resident, praying two would be a reduction, but that reduction is toward severity (since adding two is the norm), so it does not qualify as a true concession either. Thus the best phrasing is that the traveler prays the four-rak'ah fard as two. The author notes that qasr applies only to fard prayers, not to witr or sunnahs. Finally, there is a difference of opinion on performing sunnah prayers during travel: some say leaving them is better (as a concession), others say performing them is better (as an act of worship). Al-Hinduwani reconciles by recommending performing them when stopping and leaving them when traveling.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): To shorten prayer from four rak'ahs to two; in the Hanafi school, it is an obligation for the traveler.
عزيمة (azimah): An original obligation that is not a concession; here, qasr is labeled as azimah because it was the original ruling from the beginning.
رخصة (rukhsa): A concessionary ruling that eases the original obligation due to hardship; the passage argues that qasr is not a rukhsa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi madhhab consider qasr to be an 'azimah and not a rukhsa for the traveler?
A) Because the traveler has no excuse
B) Because originally prayer was prescribed as two rak'ahs for both traveler and resident, and only the resident was later increased to four
C) Because the traveler never reaches the distance requiring qasr
D) Because the hadiths are weak

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, qasr is the obligatory original ruling for the traveler, not a concession; completing four rak'ahs is sinful.

Sunnah Prayers During Travel: The Balanced View
Intra-Hanafi dispute on performing sunnah prayers during travel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on performing sunnah prayers while traveling and apply al-Hinduwani's distinction to determine the recommended practice.

Core Idea
Perform sunnah prayers when settled, omit them when moving – the balanced Hanafi view.

Scholars differ on whether a traveler should pray the sunnah prayers. Some say to abandon them as a concession, others insist on performing them. Al-Hinduwani offers a practical middle path: it depends on whether you are halting or traveling. Later Hanafi scholars like al-Ramli endorse this as the most balanced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is the sunnah prayers (rawatib) during travel. The dispute is whether it is better to perform them or leave them.
2
List the three positions
First position: better to leave them as a concession (tarkhis). Second: better to perform them for nearness (qurba). Third: al-Hinduwani distinguishes between halting and moving.
3
Note the authority endorsing the balanced view
Al-Ramli in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli says the most balanced is al-Hinduwani's view.
4
Apply to a real scenario
If you are staying at a rest stop (halting), pray the sunnah. If you are on a moving bus, you may omit them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في ترك السنن في السفر؛ فقيل الأفضل هو الترك ترخيصاً، وقيل الفعل تقرباً. وقال الهندواني : الفعل حال النزول والترك حال السير، قوله : (وقال الهندواني الخ) قال الرملي قال في شرح منية المصلي : والأعدل ما قاله الهندواني ا هـ .
And they differed concerning leaving the sunnah prayers while traveling: it is said the better is to leave them as a concession, and it is said the better is to do them as an act of drawing near (to Allah). And al-Hinduwani said: Doing them is when halting (not moving) and leaving them is when traveling. Regarding his statement: (And al-Hinduwani said etc.) al-Ramli said in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli: The most balanced is what al-Hinduwani said.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether to pray the sunnah prayers (sunans) while traveling. Some scholars say it is better to omit them as a concession (tarkhis), others say to perform them for nearness (qurba). Al-Hinduwani distinguishes: perform them when you are settled (during a halt) and leave them while actually moving. Al-Ramli endorses this as the most balanced view. The text shows that there is no single ruling; it depends on the situation.

Key Terms
سنن (sunan): The non-obligatory (sunnah) prayers, like those associated with the five daily prayers.
ترخيصاً (tarkhīṣan): as a concession; meaning it is allowed to leave the sunnah prayers due to travel
تقرباً (taqarruban): as an act of drawing near to Allah; meaning one should perform them to earn reward

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hinduwani, when should a traveler perform the sunnah prayers?
A) Only when traveling.
B) When halting (not moving).
C) Always, regardless of situation.
D) Never while traveling.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers a nuanced view on sunnah prayers during travel: the most balanced position is to perform them when halting and omit them when moving.

Traveler's Sunnah Prayers: The Criterion of Safety and Settlement
Giving the preferred view on which sunnah prayers to pray while traveling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of amn wa qarar to determine whether a traveler should perform sunnah prayers.

Core Idea
The traveler prays all sunnahs when safe and settled; omission is excused only by fear or motion.

Early Hanafi scholars disagreed on which sunnah prayers a traveler should maintain. Some limited it to Fajr; others added Maghrib. The chosen view removes the fixed list and instead provides a practical criterion: look at the traveler's situation. When he is safe and settled (amn wa qarar), the wisdom of sunnahs as completions applies. When he is in fear or actively traveling, he has a valid excuse to omit them. Understanding this criterion is key to applying the ruling in modern travel.

Guided Path
1
Recall the earlier opinions
Note the two positions: (1) pray only the sunnah of Fajr, (2) also pray the sunnah of Maghrib.
These opinions show the range of ijtihad before the preferred view.
2
Identify the chosen view (al-mukhtar)
From al-Tajnis, the preferred view is that the traveler prays the sunnahs when safe and settled, and omits them when in fear.
This view supersedes the earlier fixed-list approaches.
3
Understand the criterion: amn wa qarar
Two conditions must be met for the recommendation to apply: safety (amn) and being settled (qarar, i.e., not in motion). Fear or actual travel removes the recommendation.
The criterion is situational, not based on the number of miles traveled.
4
Apply the criterion to a scenario
Consider a traveler who stops at a hotel for the night. He is safe and stationary → he should pray the sunnahs. If he is fleeing an attack, he may omit them.
The ruling depends on the traveler's current state, not on the fact of traveling itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل يصلي سنة الفجر خاصة، وقيل سنة المغرب أيضاً. وفي التجنيس : والمختار أنه إن كان حال أمن وقرار يأتي بها لأنها شرعت مكملات والمسافر إليه محتاج، وإن كان حال خوف لا يأتي بها لأنه ترك بعذر .اهـ.
And it is said: he prays the sunnah of Fajr specifically, and it is said: also the sunnah of Maghrib. In al-Tajnis: The chosen view is that if he is in a state of security and settlement, he performs them because they were legislated as completions and the traveler is in need of them, and if he is in a state of fear, he does not perform them because omission is with an excuse.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage discusses which specific sunnah prayers a traveler should maintain. Some say only the Fajr sunnah, others add Maghrib. The preferred view from al-Tajnis is that if the traveler is safe and settled (e.g., in a camp or inn), he should perform all sunnahs as they are complementary. But if he is in a state of fear or actually traveling, he may omit them as he has an excuse. This balances the earlier dispute by providing a practical criterion.

Key Terms
أمن وقرار (amn wa qarār): A state of safety and being settled (not in motion).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view in al-Tajnis, when should a traveler perform the sunnah prayers?
A) Only when in fear.
B) When in a state of security and settlement.
C) Only at night.
D) Never during travel.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi preferred view is that a traveler prays sunnah prayers when safe and settled, and omits them only when in fear or active travel.

Number of Rak'ahs for Traveler and the Fatwa Anecdote
Stating the number of rak'ahs for traveler and a fatwa anecdote

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of obligatory rak'ahs for a resident and a traveler, explain why different answers can be correct, and apply the rule that two- and three-rak'ah prayers are not shortened.

Core Idea
Travelers shorten only the 4-rak'ah prayers, reducing daily fard from 17 to 11.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the number of obligatory rak'ahs during travel and illustrates the principle with a fascinating anecdote from 'Umdat al-Fatawa. The story shows that legal knowledge requires precision—each wife gave a different number, yet all were correct under different assumptions. Understanding this ruling is essential for every Muslim who travels.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
Determine which obligatory prayers are shortened during travel. The text says only four-rak'ah prayers (Zuhr, Asr, Isha) are shortened; two-rak'ah (Fajr) and three-rak'ah (Maghrib) remain as they are.
2
Calculate the numbers
For a resident, total fard rak'ahs = 2 (Fajr) + 4 (Zuhr) + 4 (Asr) + 3 (Maghrib) + 4 (Isha) = 17. For a traveler, each 4-rak'ah prayer becomes 2, so total = 2 + 2 + 2 + 3 + 2 = 11.
These numbers are from the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the anecdote
Each wife gave a different number, but all were correct in context: 17 for the resident, 20 includes witr, 15 refers to Friday (when Zuhr is replaced by 2-rak'ah Jumu'ah), and 11 for the traveler.
The anecdote teaches that legal questions must be answered with full context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالرباعي لأنه لا قصر في الفرض الثنائي والثلاثي فالركعات المفروضة حال الإقامة سبعة عشر، وحال السفر إحدى عشر. وفي عمدة الفتاوى للصدر الشهيد : إذا قال لنسائه من لم يدر منكن كم ركعة فرض يوم وليلة فهي طالق فقالت إحداهن عشرون ركعة والأخرى سبع عشرة ركعة والأخرى خمس عشرة والأخرى إحدى عشرة، لا تطلق واحدة منهن . أما السبع عشرة لا يشكل ومن قالت عشرون ركعة فقد ضمت الوتر إليها، ومن قالت خمس عشرة فيوم الجمعة، ومن قالت إحدى عشرة ففرض المسافر اهـ.
And he restricted to four-rak'ah (prayers) because there is no shortening in the two- or three-rak'ah obligatory prayers. So the obligatory rak'ahs while resident are seventeen, and while traveling are eleven. And in 'Umdat al-Fatawa by al-Sadr al-Shahid: If a man said to his wives: 'Any of you who does not know how many rak'ahs are obligatory per day and night is divorced,' and one said twenty, another seventeen, another fifteen, and another eleven, none of them are divorced. As for seventeen, it is not problematic; the one who said twenty included the witr; the one who said fifteen (meant) Friday (since Friday has a different count); and the one who said eleven (meant) the traveler's obligatory (prayers).
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage states that only the four-rak'ah prayers are shortened during travel; two- and three-rak'ah prayers are not. The total fard rak'ahs for a resident is 17, for a traveler 11. Then an anecdote from 'Umdat al-Fatawa illustrates that a man divorced his wives if they didn't know the count. Each gave a different number, but all were correct under different circumstances: 17 is standard resident, 20 includes witr, 15 is for Friday (since Zuhr is replaced by Jumu'ah of 2), 11 is for a traveler. None are divorced because they all understood correctly based on context.

Key Terms
الفرض الثنائي والثلاثي (al-fard al-thunā'ī wa al-thulāthī): The obligatory prayers with 2 rak'ahs (Fajr) and 3 rak'ahs (Maghrib).
الوتر (al-witr): The witr prayer, which is wajib in the Hanafi school and consists of 3 rak'ahs. It is not part of the 5 daily fard prayers but is sometimes included in the count.
المسافر (al-musāfir): A traveler, one who undertakes a journey of sufficient distance (approx. 77 km or 48 miles in the Hanafi school) that permits shortening and combining prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many obligatory rak'ahs does a traveler pray per day?
A) 17
B) 11
C) 13
D) 15

Key Takeaway
A traveler shortens only the 4-rak'ah fard prayers, making the daily total 11 rak'ahs, while 2- and 3-rak'ah prayers remain unchanged.

Intention of a Disbeliever and Qasr
Ruling on whether a child and a disbeliever may shorten prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that a disbeliever's travel intention is valid (mu'tabara) for qasr upon conversion, and contrast it with the alternative view of Imam al-Fadli.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's valid intention before Islam is considered for qasr upon conversion – but another Hanafi view says both complete prayer.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a child and a disbeliever embark on a three-day journey. After two days, the child reaches puberty and the disbeliever converts. Should they now shorten their prayers? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether the disbeliever's prior intention is legally considered. Understanding this dispute clarifies the principle of niyyat al-kāfir and its application in travel rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the passage carefully. Note: a child and a disbeliever start a journey of three days. After two days, the child becomes baligh and the disbeliever converts. The question is about their prayer length for the remaining journey.
The key variables: age (child vs adult) and religious status (disbeliever vs Muslim) at the start and after two days.
2
State the Two Positions
The passage presents two rulings: (1) according to al-Khulasa, the convert shortens (qasr) but the child does not; (2) according to Imam al-Fadli, both complete the prayer (tamam). Identify each position clearly.
The first position is labeled 'al-mukhtār' (the chosen view), indicating tarjih.
3
Understand the Legal Basis
Why does the convert shorten? The basis is that 'niyyat al-kāfir mu'tabara' – the intention of a disbeliever is considered valid for legal rulings like determining travel distance. The child's intention before puberty is not considered, so he does not qualify as a traveler.
This principle is specific to the Hanafi school and is a point of contention within the madhhab.
4
Analyze the Disagreement
Imam al-Fadli holds that both should complete prayer. His reasoning likely treats the child's intention as invalid and the disbeliever's intention as also invalid, or he requires a valid intention at the time of qasr. The disagreement shows the scope of 'mu'tabara'.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhāhib.
5
Apply the Chosen View
The text explicitly says the chosen view ('wa huwa al-mukhtār') is that the convert shortens. Thus, for fatwa, the disbeliever's prior intention suffices. The child does not shorten regardless.
The tarjih is clear: the view of al-Khulasa prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أطلق الإرادة فشملت إرادة الكافر . قال في الخلاصة : صبي ونصراني خرجا إلى سفر مسيرة ثلاثة أيام ولياليها فلما سارا يومين أسلم النصراني وبلغ الصبي، فالنصراني يقصر الصلاة فيما بقي من سفره، والصبي يتم الصلاة بناء على أن نية الكافر معتبرة وهو المختار، والإمام الجليل الفضلي سوى بينهما يعني كلاهما يتمان الصلاة اهـ .
He made the intention absolute, so it includes the intention of the disbeliever. He said in al-Khulasa: A boy and a Christian set out on a journey of three days and nights. When they had traveled two days, the Christian embraced Islam and the boy reached puberty. Then the Christian shortens prayer in the remainder of his travel, and the boy prays complete prayer based on the fact that the intention of the disbeliever is considered valid, which is the chosen view. And the great Imam al-Fadli made them equal, meaning both pray complete prayer.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario: a child and a disbeliever begin a journey. After two days, the child reaches puberty and the disbeliever converts. The question: do they now shorten prayer? According to al-Khulasa, the convert shortens because his prior travel intention as a disbeliever is considered valid (the chosen view). The child does not shorten because his intention before puberty is not considered. However, Imam al-Fadli made them equal, ruling that both complete prayer (i.e., do not shorten). This shows an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the effect of a disbeliever's intention for travel.

Key Terms
نية الكافر (niyyat al-kāfir): The intention of a disbeliever; whether it is considered valid for legal rulings like travel.
قصر (qasr): Shortening of prayer from four rak'ahs to two for a traveler.
تمام (tamām): Completing the prayer in full (not shortening).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view in al-Khulasa, after a disbeliever embraces Islam during travel, does he shorten prayers?
A) Yes, because his prior intention is considered.
B) No, he must complete his prayers.
C) He only shortens if he intended to travel before Islam.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's chosen view holds that a disbeliever's travel intention is considered valid after conversion, allowing qasr, while a child's intention is not; an alternative view by Imam al-Fadli rejects this distinction.

Completing Four Rak'ahs Without Intending Residence
Ruling on completion of four rak'ahs by a traveler

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a traveler's four-rak'ah prayer to be valid when completed, and identify when it becomes supererogatory.

Core Idea
A traveler who completes four rak'ahs must sit after two; otherwise the prayer is invalid.

In this passage, al-Marghinani explains the scenario where a traveler prays four rak'ahs without intending residence. The key is the sitting for tashahhud after two rak'ahs. If the traveler sits, the first two are the fard and the last two become supererogatory. If he does not sit, the prayer is invalid because he mixed supererogatory with the obligatory before completing it. This lesson will guide you through the conditions and consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a traveler praying four rak'ahs. The ruling depends on whether he sits after two rak'ahs. If he sits, the prayer is valid; if not, invalid.
The ruling is about validity, not a general hukm level like fard or wajib.
2
Understand the condition of sitting
Sitting for tashahhud after two rak'ahs completes the obligatory part. The last two then become supererogatory (nafl).
This is similar to how Fajr prayer is two rak'ahs; here, the first two are the fard.
3
Determine the effect of not sitting
If he does not sit, he mixes supererogatory with obligatory before completing, invalidating the fard.
The traveler becomes sinful for delaying the salam.
4
Check the recitation requirement
Recitation must be in the first two rak'ahs. If he omits recitation in the first two and recites in the last two, the fard is invalid.
This is a condition for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرض الرباعي فلو أتم وقعد في الثانية صح وإلا لا حتى يدخل مصره أو ينوي إقامة . قوله: (فلو أتم وقعد في الثانية صح وإلا (لا أي وإن لم يقعد على رأس الركعتين لم يصح فرضه لأنه إذا قعد فقد تم فرضه وصارت الأخريان له نفلاً كالفجر وصار آثماً لتأخير السلام، وإن لم يقعد فقد خلط النفل بالفرض قبل إكماله. وأشار إلى أنه لا بد أن يقرأ في الأوليين فلو ترك فيهما أو في إحداهما وقرأ في الأخريين لم يصح فرضه. وهذا كله إن لم ينو الإقامة، فإن نواها قال الأسبيجابي : لو صلى المسافر ركعتين وقرأ فيهما وتشهد ثم نوى الإقامة قبل التسليم أو بعد ما قام إلى الثالثة قبل أن يقيدها بسجدة فإنه يتحول فرضه إلى الأربع إلا أنه يعيد القيام والركوع لأنه فعله بنية التطوع فلا ينوب عن الفرض وهو مخير في القراءة، فلو قيدها بسجدة ثم نواها لم يتحول فرضه ويضيف إليها أخرى، ولو أفسدها لا شيء عليه. ولو لم يتشهد وقام إلى الثالثة ثم نوى الإقامة تحول فرضه أربعاً اتفاقاً، فإن لم يقم صلبه عاد إلى التشهد، وإن أقامه لا يعود وهو مخير في القراءة ولو قام إلى الثالثة ثم نوى قبل السجدة تحول الفرض ويعيد القيام والركوع، ولو قيد بالسجدة فقد تأكد الفساد فيضيف أخرى فتكون الأربع تطوعاً على قولهما خلافاً لمحمد، فعنده لا تنقلب بعد الفساد تطوعاً. ولو ترك القراءة وأتى بالتشهد ثم نوى الإقامة قبل أن يسلم أو قام إلى الثالثة ثم نوى الإقامة قبل أن يقيدها بالسجدة فإنه يتحول إلى الأربع ويقرأ في الأخريين قضاء عن الأوليين، ولو قيد الثالثة بسجدة ثم نوى فسدت اتفاقاً ويضيف رابعة لتكون تطوعاً عندهما ا هـ . قوله : (حتى يدخل مصره أو ينوي الإقامة نصف شهر في بلد أو (قرية) متعلق بقوله «قصر» أي قصر إلى غاية دخول المصر أو نية الإقامة في موضع صالح للمدة المذكورة فلا يقصر. أطلق في
The four-rak'ah obligatory prayer: so if he completes and sits in the second, it is valid; otherwise not, until he enters his hometown or intends residence. His statement: ('If he completes and sits in the second, it is valid; otherwise not') meaning if he does not sit at the end of the two rak'ahs, his obligatory prayer is not valid, because if he sits, his obligatory prayer is completed and the other two become supererogatory for him like Fajr, and he becomes sinful for delaying the salam. And if he does not sit, he mixed supererogatory with obligatory before completing it. And he indicated that it is necessary to recite in the first two rak'ahs; if he omits recitation in both or one of them and recites in the last two, his obligatory prayer is not valid. All this is if he has not intended residence. If he intends residence, al-Asbijabi said: If the traveler prays two rak'ahs, recites in them, and makes the tashahhud, then intends residence before the salam or after standing up for the third before tying it with a prostration, then his obligatory prayer converts to four, except that he repeats the standing and bowing because he did them with the intention of supererogatory, so they do not count for the obligatory, and he is free to choose in recitation. If he tied the third with a prostration and then intended residence, his prayer does not convert and he adds another (fourth). If he vitiated it, nothing is upon him. And if he did not make tashahhud and stood for the third then intended residence, his prayer converts to four by agreement. If he did not straighten his back, he returns to tashahhud; if he straightened it, he does not return and is free in recitation. And if he stood for the third then intended before the prostration, the prayer converts and he repeats the standing and bowing. And if he tied it with prostration, then corruption is confirmed, so he adds another, and the four become supererogatory according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Muhammad (al-Shaybani) in one narration? Actually 'on his saying' (indahu) means according to Muhammad? The text says 'خلافاً لمحمد' meaning against Muhammad, so Muhammad holds that it does not become supererogatory after corruption. If he omitted recitation and performed tashahhud then intended residence before salam or stood for third then intended before tying with prostration, it converts to four and he recites in the last two as a make-up for the first two. If he tied the third with prostration then intended, it is vitiated by agreement, and he adds a fourth to make it supererogatory according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). (End quote). His statement: ('Until he enters his hometown or intends residence for half a month in a town or village') is related to his statement 'shortens', meaning he shortens until the limit of entering his hometown or intending residence in a place suitable for the mentioned duration, so he does not shorten.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof detailing two scenarios for the traveler's prayer: (1) If he mistakenly or intentionally completes four rak'ahs without intending residence, the prayer is only valid if he sits for tashahhud after two; otherwise it is invalid. Recitation must be in the first two rak'ahs. (2) If he intends residence during the prayer, his obligation changes to four rak'ahs, with detailed rules about when the conversion occurs and what must be repeated. The text presents a thorough analysis from al-Asbijabi, including differences between Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and the author. The overall principle is that qasr ends upon entering one's hometown or intending residence for at least half a month.

Key Terms
إقامة (iqāma): Intention to reside in a place for 15 days or more, which ends the travel ruling.
قيدها بسجدة (qayyadahā bi-sajda): To tie the rak'ah by performing a prostration, making it a complete rak'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a traveler prays four rak'ahs without intending residence and does not sit after two rak'ahs?
A) The prayer is valid but he is sinful for delaying the salam.
B) The prayer is invalid because he mixed nafl with fard before completion.
C) The first two rak'ahs are valid and the last two are invalid.
D) The prayer is makruh but otherwise valid.

Key Takeaway
A traveler must sit after two rak'ahs when completing four; otherwise the fard is invalid.

The Effect of Intending Residence During Prayer
Ruling on intending residence during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a traveler's prayer transforms when intending residence during the prayer, and distinguish between the different timings of intention relative to prostration.

Core Idea
Intending residence during prayer can convert the obligation to four rak'ahs, but timing of prostration determines whether conversion occurs.

This passage delves into the detailed rulings when a traveler intends residence (iqama) while already in the middle of a shortened prayer. The transformation of the prayer from two to four rak'ahs depends on specific moments: before or after tying the third rak'ah with a prostration. The text from al-Asbijabi presents multiple scenarios and includes an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on whether vitiated rak'ahs become supererogatory. Understanding these nuances is essential for correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial act
The traveler begins praying two rak'ahs with the intention of qasr (shortening). He recites, does tashahhud, and then intends residence.
The ruling assumes the traveler has prayed two rak'ahs correctly.
2
Determine the timing of intention relative to prostration
If he intends residence before prostrating in the third rak'ah, the prayer converts to four, and he must repeat the standing and bowing of the third.
If he has already prostrated in the third, the prayer does not convert; he adds a fourth rak'ah.
3
Apply the rules for different scenarios
The text covers: intention before salam, intention after standing for third but before prostration, intention after prostration, omission of tashahhud, omission of recitation, etc.
Each scenario has a distinct ruling.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if the prayer is vitiated after tying with prostration, the four rak'ahs become supererogatory. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees; for him, they do not become supererogatory.
This difference affects the legal status of the added prayer.

Traveler's Prayer: Qasr, Itmam, and Special Cases
States rules for traveler's prayer and applies them to child/disbeliever case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shortening and completing the traveler's prayer and apply it to cases involving a disbeliever's intention.

Core Idea
A traveler who completes four rak'ahs and sits in the second fulfills the fard; the extra two become nafl.

This lesson explores the rules for a traveler's prayer according to the Hanafi school. You will learn when shortening (qasr) is recommended, what happens if you complete four rak'ahs, and how the intention of a disbeliever affects the ruling. We will also examine a famous intra-Hanafi difference on a child and a disbeliever starting a journey.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
State the rule for a traveler who completes four rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah fard: if he sits in the second rak'ah, the fard is valid and the extra two become nafl; otherwise invalid.
The ruling applies until he enters his hometown or intends to stay.
2
Understand the conditions
The traveler's concession ends when he enters his hometown or intends to stay for the prescribed period. Shortening is only for the four-rak'ah fard, not for Maghrib or Fajr.
The journey must be of three days distance.
3
Analyze the sunnah prayers
The chosen view distinguishes between security and fear: in security, pray the sunnahs; in fear, omit them.
This is from al-Tajnīs and is the mukhtar.
4
Apply to the child/disbeliever case
If a child and a disbeliever start a journey, then the disbeliever converts and the child reaches puberty en route, the convert shortens (his intention valid) while the child completes (child's intention invalid per the mukhtar).
Imam al-Faḍlī holds both complete, but the mukhtar is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَرْضُ الرُّبَاعِيُّ فَلَو أَتَمَّ وَقَعَدَ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ صَحَّ وَإِلَّا لَا حَتَّى يَدْخُلَ مِصْرَهُ أَوْ يَنْوِيَ إِقَامَةً. وَقِيلَ يُصَلِّي سُنَّةَ الْفَجْرِ خَاصَّةً، وَقِيلَ سُنَّةَ الْمَغْرِبِ أَيْضًا. وَفِي التَّجْنِيسِ : وَالْمُخْتَارُ أَنَّهُ إِنْ كَانَ حَالَ أَمْنٍ وَقَرَارٍ يَأْتِي بِهَا لِأَنَّهَا شُرِعَتْ مُكَمِّلَاتٍ وَالْمُسَافِرُ إِلَيْهِ مُحْتَاجٌ، وَإِنْ كَانَ حَالَ خَوْفٍ لَا يَأْتِي بِهَا لِأَنَّهُ تَرَكَ بِعُذْرٍ. اهـ. وَقَيَّدَ بِالرُّبَاعِيِّ لِأَنَّهُ لَا قَصْرَ فِي الْفَرْضِ الثُّنَائِيِّ وَالثُّلَاثِيِّ فَالرَّكَعَاتُ الْمَفْرُوضَةُ حَالَ الْإِقَامَةِ سَبْعَةَ عَشَرَ، وَحَالَ السَّفَرِ إِحْدَى عَشَرَ. وَفِي عُمْدَةِ الْفَتَاوَى لِلصَّدْرِ الشَّهِيدِ : إِذَا قَالَ لِنِسَائِهِ مَنْ لَمْ يَدْرِ مِنْكُنَّ كَمْ رَكْعَةً فَرْضُ يَوْمٍ وَلَيْلَةٍ فَهِيَ طَالِقٌ فَقَالَتْ إِحْدَاهُنَّ عِشْرُونَ رَكْعَةً وَالْأُخْرَى سَبْعَ عَشْرَةَ رَكْعَةً وَالْأُخْرَى خَمْسَ عَشْرَةَ وَالْأُخْرَى إِحْدَى عَشْرَةَ، لَا تَطْلُقُ وَاحِدَةٌ مِنْهُنَّ. أَمَّا السَّبْعَ عَشْرَةَ لَا يُشْكِلُ وَمَنْ قَالَتْ عِشْرُونَ رَكْعَةً فَقَدْ ضَمَّتِ الْوِتْرَ إِلَيْهَا، وَمَنْ قَالَتْ خَمْسَ عَشْرَةَ فَيَوْمَ الْجُمُعَةِ، وَمَنْ قَالَتْ إِحْدَى عَشْرَةَ فَفَرْضَ الْمُسَافِرِ اهـ. أَطْلَقَ الْإِرَادَةَ فَشَمِلَتْ إِرَادَةَ الْكَافِرِ. قَالَ فِي الْخُلَاصَةِ : صَبِيٌّ وَنَصْرَانِيٌّ خَرَجَا إِلَى سَفَرٍ مَسِيرَةَ ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ وَلَيَالِيهَا فَلَمَّا سَارَا يَوْمَيْنِ أَسْلَمَ النَّصْرَانِيُّ وَبَلَغَ الصَّبِيُّ، فَالنَّصْرَانِيُّ يُقَصِّرُ الصَّلَاةَ فِيمَا بَقِيَ مِنْ سَفَرِهِ، وَالصَّبِيُّ يُتِمُّ الصَّلَاةَ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ نِيَّةَ الْكَافِرِ مُعْتَبَرَةٌ وَهُوَ الْمُخْتَارُ، وَالْإِمَامُ الْجَلِيلُ الْفَضْلِيُّ سَوَّى بَيْنَهُمَا يَعْنِي كِلَاهُمَا يُتِمَّانِ الصَّلَاةَ اهـ.
The four-rak'ah obligatory [prayer] — if he completes [it] and sits in the second, it is valid; otherwise not, until he enters his hometown or intends to stay. And it is said he prays the sunnah of Fajr only, and it is said the sunnah of Maghrib also. And in al-Tajnīs: the chosen [view] is that if he is in a state of security and settlement he performs them because they are legislated as completers and the traveler is in need of them, and if in a state of fear he does not perform them because he left due to an excuse. End. And he restricted [the ruling] to the four-rak'ah because there is no shortening in the two-rak'ah and three-rak'ah obligatory prayers. So the obligatory rak'ahs during residence are seventeen, and during travel are eleven. And in 'Umdat al-Fatāwā by al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd: If a man said to his wives, 'Whoever among you does not know how many rak'ahs are obligatory in a day and night, she is divorced,' and one said twenty rak'ahs, another said seventeen, another said fifteen, and another said eleven, none of them is divorced. As for seventeen, it is not problematic; one who said twenty included the witr; one who said fifteen [meant] the Friday [prayer]; and one who said eleven [meant] the traveler's obligation. End. He applied [the term] intention absolutely, so it includes the intention of the disbeliever. He said in al-Khulāṣah: A boy and a Christian set out on a journey of three days and their nights. When they had traveled two days, the Christian became Muslim and the boy reached puberty. The Christian shortens the prayer in the remainder of his journey, and the boy completes the prayer, based on the fact that the intention of the disbeliever is considered, and that is the chosen view. The eminent Imam al-Faḍlī equated between them, meaning both complete the prayer. End.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā'iq. It opens with the central ruling: the obligatory prayer for a traveler is four rak'ahs that he shortens to two, but if he completes four and sits for tashahhud at the end of the second, the prayer is valid for the obligatory two, and the extra two become supererogatory. If he does not sit in the second, the prayer is invalid. This applies until he enters his hometown or intends to stay. The author then discusses the sunnah prayers for a traveler: some say only Fajr sunnah, some add Maghrib. The chosen view in al-Tajnīs distinguishes between security and fear. He then clarifies that shortening applies only to the four-rak'ah fard, not to Maghrib (three) or Fajr (two); hence the total daily obligatory rak'ahs are seventeen for a resident and eleven for a traveler. A fatwa illustrates this: if a husband threatens divorce for ignorance of the number, different answers are all acceptable because they reflect different inclusions (e.g., witr, Jumu'ah, traveler's prayer). The passage concludes with a case of a child and a disbeliever: if they start traveling, then the disbeliever converts and the child reaches puberty en route, the former shortens (since his intention to travel as a disbeliever is valid), while the child completes (since his childhood intention is not valid). There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Imam al-Faḍlī holds that both should complete.

Key Terms
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rak'ah obligatory prayer to two rak'ahs during travel.
إتمام (itmām): Performing the full four rak'ahs of the obligatory prayer while traveling.
نية الإقامة (niyyat al-iqāmah): Intention to stay in a place for fifteen days or more, which ends the traveler's concession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A traveler in a state of security intends to pray the four-rak'ah fard. According to the passage, what is the ruling on performing the sunnah prayers?
A) He must pray all sunnah prayers.
B) He should pray the sunnahs because he needs their perfection.
C) He should not pray any sunnah because of the travel.
D) He should only pray Fajr sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The traveler’s prayer rules depend on intention and conditions; the disbeliever’s intention is valid for travel concessions.

When Qasr Ends: Entering One's Hometown or Intending Residence
Explains the conditions that terminate a traveler's concession of shortening prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that end a traveler's concession of qasr and apply the rule that physical entry into the hometown is required.

Core Idea
A traveler continues shortening prayer until physically entering his hometown or intending a 15-day stay — seeing it from afar is not enough.

This passage from Bahr al-Rā'iq clarifies the precise moment when a traveler must stop shortening his prayers. The default rule is that qasr ends upon entering one's own hometown (miṣr) or upon forming a valid intention to stay 15 days in another place. However, there is an important debate among Hanafi authorities about whether simply resolving to return home after a short trip ends the travel concession, or whether one must actually cross the city limits. The commentator prefers the latter, citing the practice of Ali (ra).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two criteria for ending qasr
The text states that the traveler shortens 'until he enters his hometown or intends to stay half a month in a city or village.' These are the two causes.
2
Explore the scope of entering the hometown
Does it include entering for a need (like fetching water)? Yes, except for the latecomer (lāḥiq) who entered after the imam finished — his prayer remains unchanged. Note that entry alone ends qasr even without intention to stay.
3
Analyze the dispute on returning before three days
Some Hanafi authorities say that if a traveler has an original homeland, his resolve to return makes him a resident immediately. The commentator insists that entry is absolutely necessary, citing the hadith of Ali.
4
Understand the dalil: the hadith of Ali (ra)
The hadith narrated in Sahih al-Bukhari shows that Ali shortened even when he could see the houses of Kufa, and only stopped upon entering. This proves that physical entry is the threshold.
5
Apply the conditions for a valid intention to reside
The text lists five conditions: cessation of travel, suitable place (not sea or desert island), unity of place, definite duration (15 days), and independence of decision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حتى يدخل مصره أو ينوي الإقامة نصف شهر في بلد أو (قرية) متعلق بقوله «قصر» أي قصر إلى غاية دخول المصر أو نية الإقامة في موضع صالح للمدة المذكورة فلا يقصر. أطلق في دخول مصره فشمل ما إذا نوى الإقامة به أولاً، وشمل ما إذا كان في الصلاة كما إذا سبقه حدث وليس عنده ماء فدخله للماء إلا اللاحق إذا أحدث ودخل مصره ليتوضأ لا يلزمه الإتمام ولا يصير مقيماً بدخوله المصر. كذا في الفتاوى الظهيرية. وشمل ما إذا كان سار ثلاثة أيام أو أقل لكن المذكور في الشرح أنه يتم إذا سار أقل بمجرد العزم على الرجوع وإن لم يدخل مصره لأنه نقض للسفر قبل الاستحكام إذ هو يحتمل النقض. قال في فتح القدير : وقياسه أن لا يحل فطره في رمضان إذا كان بينه وبين بلده يومان. وفي المجتبى : لا يبطل السفر إلا بنية الإقامة أو دخول الوطن أو الرجوع قبل الثلاثة ا هـ . والمذكور في الخانية والظهيرية وغيرهما أنه إذا رجع لحاجة نسيها ثم تذكرها فإن كان له وطن أصلي يصير مقيماً بمجرد العزم على الرجوع، وإن لم يكن له وطن أصلي يقصر ا هـ. والذي يظهر أنه لا بد من دخول المصر مطلقاً لأن العلة مفارقة البيوت قاصداً مسيرة ثلاثة أيام لا استكمال سفر ثلاثة أيام بدليل ثبوت حكم السفر بمجرد ذلك فقد تمت العلة لحكم السفر فيثبت حكمه ما لم تثبت علة حكم الإقامة وروى البخاري تعليقاً أن علياً خرج فقصر وهو يرى البيوت فلما قيل له : هذه الكوفة قال : لا حتى ندخلها، يريد أنه صلى ركعتين والكوفة بمرأى منهم فقيل له إلى آخره. وقيد بنية الإقامة لأنه لو دخل بلداً ولم ينو أنه يقيم فيها خمسة عشر يوماً وإنما يقول غداً أخرج أو بعد غد أخرج حتى بقي على ذلك سنين قصر . وفي المجتبى : والنية إنما تؤثر بخمس شرائط : أحدها ترك السير حتى لو نوى الإقامة وهو يسير لم يصح . وثانيها صلاحية الموضع حتى لو أقام في بحر أو جزيرة لم تصح واتحاد الموضع والمدة والاستقلال بالرأي اهـ. وأطلق النية فشمل الحكمية كما لو وصل الحاج إلى الشام وعلم أن القافلة إنما تخرج بعد خمسة عشر يوماً وعزم أن لا يخرج إلا معهم لا يقصر لأنه كناوي الإقامة. كذا في المحيط. وشمل ما إذا نواها في خلال الصلاة في الوقت فإنه يتم، سواء كان في أولها أو وسطها أو في آخرها وسواء كان منفرداً أو مقتدياً أو مدركاً أو مسبوقاً. أما اللاحق إذا أدرك أول الصلاة والإمام مسافر فأحدث أو نام فانتبه بعد فراغ الإمام ونوى الإقامة لم يتم لأن اللاحق في الحكم كأنه خلف الإمام، فإذا فرغ الإمام فقد استحكم الفرض فلا يتغير في قوله : (إذ هو يحتمل النقض أي لأنه لم يتم علة فكانت الإقامة نقضاً للعارض لا ابتداء علة الإتمام، ولو قيل العلة مفارقة البيوت قاصداً مسيرة ثلاثة أيام لاستكمال سفر ثلاثة أيام بدليل ثبوت حكم السفر بمجرد ذلك فقد تمت العلة لحكم السفر فيثبت حكمه ما لم يثبت علة حكم الإقامة احتاج إلى الجواب. كذا في الفتح، وعن هذا الإشكال نشأ قول المؤلف الآتي والذي يظهر الخ» قال في النهر مجيباً. وأنت خبير بأن إبطال الدليل لمعنى لا يستلزم إبطال المدلول قوله : ( وروى البخاري الخ)
His statement: (until he enters his hometown or intends to stay half a month in a city or village) is related to his saying 'qasr', i.e., he shortens until the end of entering the hometown or intention to stay in a place suitable for the mentioned period, so he does not shorten. He is absolute in entering his hometown, so it includes whether he intended to stay there at first, and includes if he is in prayer, such as if an event preceded him and he has no water so he enters it for water. Except for the one who comes later (lāḥiq) if he commits an event and enters his hometown to perform wuḍū', he is not required to complete nor does he become resident by entering his hometown. As in al-Fatāwā al-Ẓuhayriyyah. And it includes if he traveled three days or less, but it is mentioned in the commentary that he completes if he traveled less with just the resolve to return even if he did not enter his hometown, because it is a nullification of travel before it is established, since it is subject to nullification. He said in Fatḥ al-Qadīr: Its analogy is that he is not allowed to break his fast in Ramadan if there are two days between him and his city. And in al-Mujtabā: Travel is not invalidated except by intention to reside, entering the homeland, or returning before three days. End. And it is mentioned in al-Khāniyyah and al-Ẓuhayriyyah and others that if he returns for a forgotten need and then remembers, if he has an original homeland, he becomes resident merely by resolving to return; if he does not have an original homeland, he shortens. End. And what appears is that entering the hometown is absolutely necessary, because the cause is leaving houses with the intention of three days' journey, not completing a three-day travel, evidenced by the establishment of travel rules merely by that. So the cause for travel rule is complete, so its rule persists as long as the cause for residence rule is not established. And Bukhari narrated in a suspended form that Ali went out and shortened while he could see the houses, and when it was said to him: 'This is Kufa,' he said: 'No, not until we enter it,' meaning he prayed two rak'ahs while Kufa was in their sight, so it was said to him etc. And he restricted to intention of residence because if he enters a city but does not intend to stay there fifteen days, rather says 'I will go out tomorrow or the day after,' even if he remains like that for years, he shortens. And in al-Mujtabā: Intention only has effect with five conditions: first, cessation of travel; so if he intends residence while traveling, it is not valid. Second, the suitability of the place; so if he stays in a sea or an island, it is not valid. And unity of place and duration and independence of opinion. End. And he applied [the term] intention broadly, so it includes constructive intention, as when a pilgrim reaches Syria and knows that the caravan will only depart after fifteen days and resolves not to leave except with them; he does not shorten because he is like one intending residence. As in al-Muḥīṭ. And it includes if he intends it during the prayer in the time, then he completes, whether at the beginning, middle, or end, and whether he is praying alone, as an imam, a follower, or a latecomer. As for the one who joins late (lāḥiq) if he catches the beginning of the prayer while the imam is traveling, then he commits an event or sleeps, then wakes up after the imam finishes and intends residence, he does not complete because the latecomer is in the ruling like being behind the imam. So when the imam finishes, the obligation is firmly established and does not change in his statement: (Because it is subject to nullification) i.e., because the cause did not complete, so residence is a nullification of an incidental condition, not an initiation of the cause for completion. And if it were said that the cause is leaving houses intending a three-day journey to complete a three-day travel, based on the establishment of travel rules merely by that, then the cause for travel rule is complete, so its rule persists until the cause for residence rule is established, it would need an answer. Likewise in al-Fatḥ, and from this difficulty arose the author's later statement 'and what appears etc.' He said in al-Nahr in response: And you are aware that invalidating the evidence due to a meaning does not necessitate invalidating the conclusion. His statement: (And Bukhari narrated etc.)
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion on the conditions that end the traveler's concession of qasr. The commentator explains that qasr ceases upon entering one's hometown (misr) or intending to stay for half a month (15 days) in a suitable place. He clarifies that even if one enters the hometown during prayer for a reason (like getting water), the prayer is not automatically completed—except for a latecomer (lāḥiq) who entered after the imam finished, as his prayer is already firmly established. There is a dispute about traveling less than three days and then deciding to return: some say the traveler completes immediately upon resolve, others say only upon entering the hometown. The commentator prefers the view that entering the hometown is absolutely necessary, citing the hadith of Ali (ra) who continued to shorten until he entered Kufa. The passage also outlines the five conditions for a valid intention to reside: cessation of travel, suitable place, unity of place, definite duration, and independent decision. A constructive intention (e.g., a pilgrim who knows he must stay 15 days) is treated like explicit intention. The latecomer scenario emphasizes that the prayer's obligation is fixed once the imam completes, so a later intention cannot change it.

Key Terms
مصر (miṣr): One's hometown or the main city of residence; entering it terminates travel concessions.
نية الإقامة (niyyat al-iqāmah): Intention to stay in a place for 15 days or more, which turns the traveler into a resident.
لاحق (lāḥiq): A latecomer who joins the congregation after the imam has started; his ruling is like that of the imam's follower in some respects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence that a traveler must physically enter his hometown to end qasr?
A) A verse from the Quran
B) The consensus of the companions
C) The action of Ali ibn Abi Talib narrated in Bukhari
D) An analogy (qiyas) from the rules of fasting

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view is that qasr ends only when the traveler physically enters his hometown or intends a 15-day stay, not upon mere resolve or sight.
